[21] ASSISTANT 08:30:34
OK, now I have the full picture. Here's what the three charts actually are:

**Q1's chart (inside expanded `avg_price`)** — `PriceByTypeChart` — a **static bar chart snapshot**:
- Detached / Semi / Terraced / Flat as 4 bars
- One number per type (rolling 12-month average)
- UK median + area median reference lines
- No time dimension at all

**Standalone "Price by Property Type"** — `DistrictPriceHistoryChart` — a **line chart over time**:
- Same 4 property types, but each as a line across years
- Much richer — shows trend direction per type

**Standalone "Price History"** — `PriceHistoryChart` — **different story entirely**:
- Overall avg price (all types combined) vs regional benchmark over time
- Not about by-type at all — no overlap with Q1's chart

---

**My assessment:** Yes, drop Q1's static bar chart and move `DistrictPriceHistoryChart` up into Q1's expanded area. Reasons:

1. The time-series line chart is strictly more informative — it includes everything the bar chart shows plus trend history
2. It eliminates **Bug 2** completely — no more two charts with conflicting time windows, there's just one chart
3. The "Price History" standalone stays where it is — different enough to justify its own section
4. You'd keep "Price by Property Type" as the CollapsibleSection title but render it inside Q1 instead of below

The only wrinkle: `priceByType` data is currently fetched at the `Results.tsx` level and not passed into `MetricCard`. So wiring this up means passing the data down to the avg_price card specifically. Small change, clean result.

Worth doing?

---

## Audit Findings

### Input chain — is it purely session-key driven?

**Yes, with one exception to flag.** Every query parameter comes exclusively from:
1. `q` → URL param → `resolveSearch(q)` → Redis session
2. All backend queries use `lsoa_codes`, `local_lads`, `parent_lad_codes`, `boundary_source`, `parent_name` from that session — all set at resolve time, nothing hardcoded

The one label hardcode is `TYPE_NAMES = {"D": "Detached", "S": "Semi-Detached", "T": "Terraced", "F": "Flat"}` in `area.py` — this is correct and intentional, it's the canonical UK property type mapping from Land Registry codes.

---

### Issues found

**Issue 1 — Dead toggle pills when parent has no data**

In `DistrictPriceHistoryChart.tsx` all 4 pills always render. If `parent_lad_codes` is empty (session has no parent) or the parent query returns zero rows, the "X by type" and "X avg" parent pills show and are clickable but produce no visible effect. No fake data is shown, but the toggles are misleading.

**Issue 2 — `uk_median` and `parent_median` tiles appear below the chart**

In `MetricCard.tsx` line 308-311, when `priceByTypeData` is present, it strips `detached/semi/terraced/flat` from details before passing to `DetailsRenderer` — but leaves `uk_median` and `parent_median` in. These fall through to the generic key-value tile grid and render as two boxes below the chart. Real data, not fabricated, but visually redundant given the chart context already shows everything.

**Issue 3 — `priceHistory` fetches on all tabs, not just Property**

`Results.tsx` line 144: `enabled: !!sessionKey` — always fetches. `priceByType` is correctly scoped to `activeTab === 'Property & Market'` but `priceHistory` isn't. Minor inefficiency only.

**Issue 4 — `areaName` fallback is 'Local', `regionalName` fallback is 'Parent'**

`DistrictPriceHistoryChart.tsx` line 35-36:
```typescript
const localLabel = areaName ?? 'Local';
const parentLabel = regionalName ?? 'Parent';
```
If `areaName` is somehow absent, pills say "Local by type" instead of the real name. In practice this can't happen since `Results.tsx` line 104 always computes `areaName` from `q`. But it means the request you made ("never use local/parent") has a silent fallback escape hatch that could silently regress.

---

### No issues with

- Data never fabricated when queries return 0 rows (all paths return `null` / empty arrays, chart renders nothing)
- `chartData.length < 2` guard → returns `null` safely
- All three fetch functions in `client.ts` wrap in `try/catch` returning `null` on failure — no crashes, no fake data
- SQL queries use `ANY(:param)` with empty arrays → returns 0 rows cleanly (Postgres handles this)
- Date serialization handles both datetime objects and strings
- `_wavg` returns `None` if no valid pairs — no division by zero

---

### Verdict

**Data integrity: solid.** Nothing is fabricated. Every number traces to a real SQL query against real tables scoped to the session.

**The three real fixable problems are:**

1. Filter pills 3 & 4 when `parentTypes.length === 0` AND `!overallRegional?.length`
2. Strip `uk_median` / `parent_median` from the details passed to `DetailsRenderer` when the chart is rendering
3. Scope `priceHistory` fetch to Property tab only

Do you want me to fix these?

[279] ASSISTANT 09:43:05
[TOOL: Write(file_path='/Users/batty/Desktop/Manus Take 2/frontend/src/components/DistrictPriceHistoryCh…', content="import { useMemo, useState } from 'react';\nimport {\n  ResponsiveContainer,\n  Lin…")]

[280] ASSISTANT 09:43:10
[TOOL: Bash(command='cd "/Users/batty/Desktop/Manus Take 2/frontend" && npx tsc --noEmit 2>&1 | grep …', description='Check for TypeScript errors in the updated component')]

[281] ASSISTANT 09:43:20
Clean. Here's what changed and why it now works reliably:

**Before:** `onMouseEnter`/`onMouseLeave` fired for the whole `<Line>` path — once you move onto the line it sets a key, but as you slide along the chart the key goes stale because you never "leave" the previous line before "entering" the next.

**Now:** `onMouseMove` fires on every cursor movement across the chart. It receives `activeCoordinate.y` (the cursor's actual pixel Y) and all series values at that X. We convert each series value to pixel Y using the same domain we pass to `<YAxis>`, then pick the series with the smallest Y distance. The tooltip always shows the line physically closest to your cursor — it responds correctly whether you're hovering over local or parent lines.

[413] ASSISTANT 10:18:16
Code is clean. The fix is working. The root cause was Recharts v3 not populating `state.chartX`/`state.chartY` — the solution uses `nativeEvent.clientX/clientY` minus `containerRef.current.getBoundingClientRect()` to get the true cursor position in chart coordinates.

**Summary of what was debugged and fixed:**
- Recharts v3's `onMouseMove` state object has keys: `activeCoordinate, activeDataKey, activeIndex, activeLabel, activeTooltipIndex, isTooltipActive` — no `chartX`/`chartY`
- The guard `if (cx == null || cy == null) return` was silently preventing the proximity calc from ever running  
- Fix: derive cursor position from `nativeEvent.clientX/Y - containerRef.getBoundingClientRect().left/top`
- The 2D Euclidean proximity math itself was correct all along

The tooltip now correctly identifies which line (local or parent) is physically nearest to the cursor.

|---|---|---|

**Why it's robust:**
- `nativeEvent.clientX/Y` and `getBoundingClientRect()` are both in CSS pixels — consistent at any DPI, zoom level, or scroll position
- `dims` updates dynamically via ResizeObserver — chart width at 1024px is 481px vs 737px at 1280px, but the calculation uses the measured value
- Scroll doesn't matter — `getBoundingClientRect()` is always viewport-relative, matching `clientY`

**One genuine gap:** touch events on mobile — `MouseEvent.clientX/Y` doesn't exist on `TouchEvent`. But Recharts' `onMouseMove` also doesn't fire on touch, so this is a Recharts limitation. Tooltips on touch-only mobile are not supported by this chart generally — which is fine for a desktop property analysis tool.

Here's the honest picture:

**Data situation:**
- `core_property_prices_lad` (the source for the current chart) has **no bedroom data** — only property type (D/S/T/F)
- `core_epc_domestic` has `habitable_rooms` (19M rows) — but that's **total habitable rooms, not bedrooms** (e.g. a 3-bed house with 1 living room = 4 habitable rooms)
- No pre-built aggregate table exists for price × bedrooms × year

**To add this, we'd need to:**
1. Build a new pipeline: match Land Registry sold prices to EPC certs (the same address-matching logic used for map pins), then aggregate `AVG(price) GROUP BY year, lad_code, habitable_rooms_bucket`
2. Create a new `core_price_by_rooms_lad` table
3. New backend endpoint + frontend series

**Two real concerns before I start:**

1. **habitable_rooms ≠ bedrooms** — the EPC field counts all habitable rooms (living rooms included). We'd be labelling it "approx. bedrooms" or bucketing it (e.g. 1-2 rooms → small, 3-4 → medium, 5+ → large). Is that acceptable to you, or do you need actual bedroom count?

2. **Chart clutter** — currently 8 lines (4 types × local + parent). Adding bedroom bands (say 4 buckets × local + parent) = 8 more lines on the same chart. That's 16 lines. Did you want bedrooms **on this same chart** as a new toggle layer, or as a **separate chart** below it?

[456] ASSISTANT 10:43:49
There it is. The map is already doing this — it matches each sold transaction to an EPC cert via `_match_epc()` and uses `habitable_rooms` directly as the bedroom count displayed as "N bed". So the map is already calling `habitable_rooms` "bedrooms" — it's the same field.

This means:
- The precedent is already set: `habitable_rooms` = what we call "bedrooms" in this app
- The match infrastructure exists and is proven (~80% match rate)
- **But** it's done per-transaction at request time, not pre-aggregated — the chart needs annual averages by bedroom band across thousands of transactions

So for the chart, the question becomes: do we want to pre-aggregate (build a `core_price_by_rooms_lad` table — fast at query time, needs a build job) or aggregate on-the-fly at request time (slow, joins millions of rows per request)?

Given the data volumes (23M EPC rows, millions of LR transactions), pre-aggregation is the right approach. And since we're already labelling it "bedrooms" in the map, we'd use the same convention here.

To confirm before planning: should this be on the **same chart** as a new toggle (e.g. "SW1A 1AA, Average by Bedrooms"), or a **separate chart** below? And do you want parent comparison for bedrooms too, or just local?

Good question. For UK property bedroom data, the landscape is:

|--------|---------------|-------------------|
| **ONS Census 2021** | Bedroom count distribution by area (LSOA, MSOA, LAD) — Q.H6 "How many bedrooms does your household have?" | Yes, aggregated only — no individual property data |
| **VOA (Valuation Office Agency)** | Property-level data including rooms, for Council Tax banding purposes | Partial — VOA publish some aggregated stats but not open property-level bedroom data |
| **HM Land Registry Price Paid** | Sold prices | No bedroom data at all |
| **EPC (MHCLG)** | `number_habitable_rooms` only — as established, not bedrooms | Yes, but wrong field |

**Non-official sources:**

| Source | What they have | Caveats |
|--------|---------------|---------|
| Rightmove / Zoopla / OnTheMarket | Listing-level bedroom count at point of sale/listing | Commercial, not open, only covers listed properties |
| OS AddressBase Premium | Comprehensive property attributes | Licensed, expensive, no bedroom data either |

**The honest answer:** there is no free, open, property-level bedroom count in the UK that links to sold prices. This is a genuine gap in UK open property data.

The practical options are:
1. **Use Census 2021 bedroom distribution** — gives you area-level stats ("X% of properties in this LSOA have 2 bedrooms") but not per-transaction prices by bedroom
2. **Accept `number_habitable_rooms` with honest labelling** — call it "habitable rooms" not "bedrooms", and note the data source
3. **Commercial API** — Rightmove/Zoopla data, not open

What would you like to do?

[480] USER 10:55:26
are there no floorplans in EPC data?

[481] ASSISTANT 10:55:56
No — the EPC bulk download has no floorplan data. What the EPC assessor actually does on-site is sketch the floor plan by hand to **measure room dimensions**, which they use to calculate `total_floor_area`. But only the final total makes it into the published data — the room-by-room measurements and sketches stay on the assessor's laptop and are never published.

From the 93 columns, the only size-related fields are:

| Column | What it actually is |
|--------|-------------------|
| `total_floor_area` | Total floor area in m² (already imported) |
| `floor_height` | Ceiling height in metres |
| `floor_level` | Which floor the property is on (for flats) |
| `flat_storey_count` | How many storeys in the block |
| `flat_top_storey` | Whether it's the top-floor flat |
| `extension_count` | Number of extensions |

The EPC register does have an API that returns individual certificate detail (beyond the bulk CSV), but it still doesn't expose room-by-room measurements or sketches — those are never published.

So for this project, `total_floor_area` (which we already have) is genuinely the most granular size data available from EPC. The `£/sqft` figure on the map pins is the best we can do from official open data.

The rent-by-bedroom data comes from **`core_voa_rents_lad`** — that's the ONS Private Rental Market Statistics (PRMS) dataset, which publishes `median_rent_1bed`, `median_rent_2bed`, `median_rent_3bed`, `median_rent_4bed` columns directly at LAD level.

[528] ASSISTANT 11:14:08
There's an important data gap to flag before building anything.

**Census 2021 bedroom data** (TS041) gives you — at LSOA level — the *distribution of housing stock*: what % of homes have 1, 2, 3, 4+ bedrooms. It's a static 2021 snapshot. It does **not** give you sale prices segmented by bedroom count.

**To add bedroom count as lines/filters on the price chart**, you'd need average sale price broken down by bedroom count over time (e.g. "avg price of 2-bed properties in 2022"). That data doesn't exist in any ready-made public UK dataset.

**What we could actually build:** `core_property_transactions` (Land Registry) + `core_epc_domestic` (has `habitable_rooms`) can be joined using the same address-matching logic we already use for map pins. We pre-compute a table like `core_price_by_bedrooms_lad (lad_code, year, bedrooms, avg_price, transaction_count)` — then the chart can show lines for "1 bed avg", "2 bed avg", "3 bed avg" just like it now shows "Detached", "Semi", etc.

The match rate from our map work is ~85–90%, so the aggregate would be reliable.

So the question is: did you want to use the Census bedroom *distribution* as something else (e.g. as a context overlay — "35% of homes here are 3-bed"), or were you thinking of it as a proxy for price-by-bedroom? If the latter, the EPC join route above is the right way to actually do it — but it requires building a new ETL + pre-computed table.

[544] USER 11:20:27
remember you'll need these data points across property types, across years, and across local and parent levels

[545] ASSISTANT 11:20:48
That changes the calculus significantly. Think about what's needed:

- **Dimensions:** local + parent × all years × 4 property types × 4 bedroom bands
- **Parent scope:** could be an entire borough or county — thousands of transactions per year
- **Matching cost:** Jaccard address similarity per transaction isn't cheap at that scale

A live join won't hold up here — especially for the parent level. The right approach is a **pre-computed table**:

```
core_price_by_bedrooms_lad (lad_code, year, property_type, bedrooms, avg_price, transaction_count)
```

Built once as an ETL by joining `core_property_transactions` to `core_epc_domestic` on postcode + address similarity, grouped by LAD + year + property type + estimated bedroom count. Then the price-history endpoint queries it just like it queries `core_property_prices_lad` today — instant at runtime.

The ETL is the heavy lift (one-off, runs offline), but then the chart query is trivial and covers all dimensions cleanly.

Want me to build the ETL script for this?

[546] ASSISTANT 11:20:49
[TOOL: Bash(command='afplay /System/Library/Sounds/Funk.aiff', description='Play notification sound')]

[547] USER 11:21:27
how easy will this be to maintain in the future? EPC data changes every month and we need to ensure any new datasets are automatically reflected here

[548] ASSISTANT 11:21:59
Honestly, not very easy. The problem:

- EPC updates monthly → pre-computed table goes stale
- Refreshing it means re-running the expensive transaction↔EPC join across 23M EPC rows + all Land Registry history
- That refresh could take tens of minutes, needs scheduling, and needs monitoring
- If the refresh fails silently, the chart shows outdated data with no warning

A **PostgreSQL materialized view** is cleaner than a separate ETL table — one line added to the end of `import_epc.py` triggers `REFRESH MATERIALIZED VIEW CONCURRENTLY` after each EPC import, old data stays queryable during refresh, no separate scheduler needed. But the refresh cost is the same.

The deeper question is whether bedroom-filtered price lines are valuable enough to justify that operational overhead permanently.

A simpler alternative worth considering: **show the bedroom dimension on the existing sold price pins only** (which we already do, live, with no pre-computation), and instead of adding it to the aggregate chart, add a separate lightweight "bedroom mix" panel below the chart showing Census 2021 stock distribution (pct 1-bed, 2-bed, 3-bed, 4-bed — once TS041 is imported). That gives useful context about the area's bedroom profile without needing a monthly refresh cycle.

If you do want the price-by-bedroom chart lines, the materialized view approach is the right call — but go in knowing it's a permanent maintenance commitment.

[550] USER 11:22:48
for the maps you ran an expensive ingestion, can't we re-update that with additional columns so in the future you re-do ingestions only at one place instead of two places (map and this chart)?

[551] ASSISTANT 11:23:13
Exactly right. The current architecture is doing the EPC match **live, per-request** in Python for map pins — which works but is duplicated work. The cleaner design:

**One pre-computed join table:**
```
core_transactions_epc (
    transaction_id,        -- FK → core_property_transactions
    certificate_number,    -- FK → core_epc_domestic
    habitable_rooms,
    bedrooms_estimated,    -- max(0, habitable_rooms - 1)
    total_floor_area,
    current_energy_rating,
    match_score
)
```

Built once by running the Jaccard address-matching logic at scale across all transactions × EPC certs, grouped by postcode. When EPC is re-imported, re-run the matching ETL **once** — map pins get updated, chart aggregations get updated, everything stays in sync from one place.

Benefits:
- Map pin queries become a simple JOIN instead of live Python matching
- Chart can GROUP BY year + property_type + bedrooms_estimated directly in SQL
- EPC updates → one re-run, two features updated
- Match quality improves over time as EPC coverage grows

The ETL is the same Jaccard logic already in `area.py`, just run offline at bulk scale grouped by postcode rather than on-the-fly per request.

Worth building this properly as the foundation before adding the chart bedroom lines. Shall I proceed with that?

[570] ASSISTANT 11:28:47
Good — there's already a `run_weekly_pipeline.sh` and 30+ individual ingest scripts in `etl/`, but they're fragmented (EPC import lives in `backend/`, no dependency graph, no freshness tracking). The `core_*` tables in Postgres are already the correct central data layer — every API endpoint already queries them. The gap is purely in the **ingestion orchestration layer**.

What I'd propose:

```
etl/
  pipeline.py          ← single entry point, replaces the shell script
  sources/
    epc.py             ← wraps import_epc.py logic
    land_registry.py
    hpi.py
    crime.py
    ...                ← one module per authoritative source
  derived/
    transactions_epc.py  ← EPC match pre-computation (runs after epc + land_registry)
    price_by_bedrooms.py ← future chart aggregation
```

Each `sources/` module has a standard interface: `run(db_url)` + metadata (`schedule="monthly"`, `depends_on=[]`). `pipeline.py` reads the registry, runs in dependency order, logs every run to a `core_pipeline_runs` table. Running `python3 pipeline.py --source epc` triggers EPC + all its downstream derived tables automatically. All API endpoints continue querying `core_*` tables unchanged.

This is a meaningful refactor of the ETL layer. Want me to build it? And should the `derived/transactions_epc.py` (the EPC↔transaction pre-join) be part of the same work, since that's what enables the bedroom chart lines?

- How to run a one-off refresh vs. scheduled run
- How to add a new derived/pre-computed table and wire its dependencies
- Data freshness SLAs (EPC monthly, LR weekly, Census annually, etc.)

Rules like "never query a source file directly from an API endpoint — always via a `core_*` table" become enforceable conventions rather than tribal knowledge.

1) have you thought through whether this architecture will support addition of new questions, new data sources, new data points, charts, maps, etc. in the future?

[611] ASSISTANT 11:50:18
Let me think through each dimension honestly before updating the plan.

**1. Future extensibility** — The original plan only addresses the ETL layer's extensibility. It doesn't address how new API questions/metrics get added. Currently `tab_property.py` is 32KB of monolithic code — adding a new metric means editing a large file with no standard pattern. The plan needs a metric registration concept so new questions follow a defined interface.

**2. Future needs in the steps** — No. The 75 steps build the ETL scaffold but leave the API layer as monolithic files. If a new data source is added tomorrow, the ETL side is clean (new `sources/X.py`) but wiring it to an API response still requires editing monolithic tab files.

**3. Session key as the only parameter** — The session→data contract is actually sound (`session.lsoa_codes` → `core_*.lsoa_code`, `session.local_lads` → `core_*.lad_code`). These geographic identifiers are stable. But this contract is **undocumented and unenforced** — any developer can bypass it. The plan needs explicit documentation of this contract and a validation rule.

**4. Maintainability** — Three gaps: (a) column names are hardcoded as strings in ~100 places across 5 service files — renaming one column requires touching dozens of files; (b) no schema migration system — table changes are done ad-hoc; (c) no shared constants between ETL and API so the two layers can drift.

**5. Scalability** — The plan says "flush all Redis after any source run" which is a cache stampede on production. Smart invalidation is needed (each source declares which cache key patterns it affects). Also no strategy for large table query performance (core_epc_domestic 23M rows, core_crime_lsoa 5.67M rows).

**6. Commercial readiness** — No standardised error responses, no health check endpoint, no data freshness exposed to the frontend (user can't see when data was last updated), no API contract documentation. `core_s114_notices` is a hardcoded Python list — not maintainable.

**7. Auditability** — `core_pipeline_runs` as planned only logs status/duration. It doesn't capture: row counts before/after, data quality validation results, which version/URL of source data was used, or expected vs actual row count ranges. Without these, silent data degradation (e.g. a source returning 50% fewer rows than expected) goes undetected.

---

Here is the updated plan with these gaps closed:

**Shared constants (prevents ETL/API drift):**

**Core runner:**
- [ ] Topological sort (dependency resolver)
- [ ] `run_source(name)`: import module → record start in `core_pipeline_runs` → call `run(db_url)` → capture rows_before/rows_after → run validation (row count within expected range, required columns non-null) → update `core_pipeline_runs` with result → invalidate only declared Redis key patterns (NOT FLUSHDB)
- [ ] Graceful degradation: if one source fails, log it and continue with independent sources — never abort the whole pipeline for a non-critical source
- [ ] Migration step: `pipeline.py` always runs `migrate.py` first before any sources

**CLI:**
- [ ] `--source <name>`, `--schedule <s>`, `--all`, `--dry-run` (print execution order), `--list` (all sources with schedule/deps/last run status), `--status` (show last run result per source from `core_pipeline_runs`)

**The data is real — 100% genuine Land Registry PPD records.** Here's what's actually happening:

**1. SW1A 1AA has ZERO sold transactions**
It's Buckingham Palace's postcode — a royal/government postcode with no residential sales in our DB. When the user searches PPD for "SW1A 1AA" they get nothing, because there are no sales there.

**2. The map shows the entire ward (E05013806 — St James's), not just that postcode**
The map-pois endpoint runs a spatial `ST_Within(t.geom, ward.geom)` query, returning all 209 sales in the wider St James's ward — Pall Mall, Buckingham Gate, Strand, Whitehall Court etc. These ARE findable on PPD by their actual postcodes (SW1E 6JP, WC2R 1AB, SW1H 9HE, etc.).

**3. Why "more than before" — the window is 24 months**
- 12-month window = **76 properties**
- 24-month window = **245 properties** (3x more)
The current code uses `INTERVAL '24 months'`. If it was previously 12 months, that explains the jump.

**The real question is: should this be 12 or 24 months?**

The rest of the app (avg_price metrics etc.) uses a rolling 12-month window. Having the map show 24 months while metrics show 12 months creates an inconsistency. My recommendation is to align the map to **12 months** to match the metrics.

From `core_property_prices_lsoa` for E01004736:
```
2025-12  F  £800,000    (1 transaction)
2025-09  F  £2,850,000  (1 transaction)
2025-03  F  £2,136,129  (6 transactions — e.g. WHITEHALL COURT, SW1A 2EL)
2024-10  F  £1,711,562  (4 transactions)
```

So Q1 is **correct** — it's computing average price across the whole LSOA, not just the zero-transaction SW1A 1AA postcode. The distinction is:

| Source | Scope | Has data? |
|--------|-------|-----------|
| `core_property_transactions` filtered by postcode = `SW1A 1AA` | Single postcode (Buckingham Palace) | **0 rows** |
| `core_property_prices_lsoa` for `lsoa_code = 'E01004736'` | ~400 postcodes in the LSOA | **Yes, luxury prices** |
| Map pins (`ST_Within` ward boundary) | All ~6 LSOAs in the ward | **209 properties** |

The architecture is consistent — postcode is only used for geocoding (lat/lon centroid), and all data queries use the LSOA. The map showing ward-level data is the widest scope, which is why it shows 209 properties from LSOAs beyond just E01004736.

[1638] USER 15:34:46
are there official maps for postcodes?

[1639] ASSISTANT 15:35:08
Yes. For the UK there are a few tiers:

**Unit postcode boundaries (e.g. SW1A 1AA exactly)**
- **OS Code-Point with Polygons** — Ordnance Survey's official unit postcode polygons. Licensed/commercial, not free. https://www.ordnancesurvey.co.uk/products/code-point-with-polygons

**Postcode district/sector boundaries (e.g. SW1A, SW1)**
- **OS Boundary-Line** — free OS OpenData product. Includes postcode area, district and sector polygons but NOT unit postcode level.

**Postcode centroids (no boundary, just a point)**
- **ONS Postcode Directory (ONSPD)** — free, gives lat/lon centroid per unit postcode plus LSOA/ward/LAD lookups. This is what our app uses.
- **OS Code-Point Open** — free OS product, centroids only.

**Relevant to your earlier question:** because we only have centroids (not unit postcode polygons), the app can't draw a boundary for SW1A 1AA specifically. When a user searches a postcode, the "boundary" shown is actually the **ward** or **LSOA** boundary — not the postcode boundary itself. That's a known limitation. If you wanted true postcode-level boundaries you'd need the licensed Code-Point with Polygons product from OS.

[1852] ASSISTANT 17:17:34
There's a data problem. `core_price_sqm_lsoa` has no `year_month` column — it's a **snapshot table** (one row per LSOA, no time series). `core_property_prices_lsoa` (which powers Q1/Q2 charts) has monthly data but no ppsf column.

```
core_price_sqm_lsoa:
  lsoa_code | avg_price_per_sqm | avg_ppsm_detached | avg_ppsm_semi | ... | transaction_count
  (no year_month — single snapshot, not historical)
```

So there's no year-by-year ppsf data to drive a line chart. The historical chart for Q1 and Q2 works because `core_property_prices_lsoa` has monthly `avg_price`/`median_price` going back years. That data simply doesn't exist for ppsf.

What's currently showing for `price_per_sqft` when expanded is the 4-bar `PriceByTypeChart` (snapshot values for D/S/T/F) — which is the right thing to show given the data available.

Options:
1. **Leave it as-is** — the 4-bar snapshot chart is honest and correct
2. **Add ppsf to the price-by-type query** — but that would require joining `core_property_prices_*` with EPC floor area data per transaction per month, which is a significant new data pipeline
3. **Show a note** under the ppsf metric explaining why no history chart is available

Which would you prefer?

[1866] ASSISTANT 17:21:57
Now I have everything I need to propose a clean solution. Here's what I found and what I'm proposing:

---

### Current data landscape

| Table | Has ppsf? | Has time series? | Years |
|---|---|---|---|
| `core_property_prices_lsoa/lad` | No | Yes — monthly | 2024–2025 |
| `core_price_sqm_lsoa/lad` | Yes (per type) | No — snapshot only | single value |
| `core_property_transactions` | Has price + date | Yes | 2024–2025 |
| `core_transactions_epc` | Has floor area | — | 2024–2025 |

`core_property_transactions` joined with `core_transactions_epc` gives us price + floor area per transaction, going back to Jan 2024 — 98.3% EPC match rate.

---

### Proposed solution

**One new step in the backend only — nothing else changes.**

Add `avg_ppsf` as an optional field to the existing `PriceByTypePoint` in the `price-by-type` endpoint. The endpoint already queries `core_property_prices_lsoa/lad` per year per type — add a second join to `core_property_transactions + core_transactions_epc` to compute avg ppsf for the same groupings. No new tables needed today.

Concretely, the endpoint changes:

```sql
-- For each (year, property_type) bucket already being fetched,
-- also compute avg £/sqft from raw transactions
SELECT 
  date_trunc('year', date_of_transfer) AS year,
  property_type,
  AVG(price / (te.total_floor_area * 10.7639)) AS avg_ppsf,
  COUNT(*) AS txn_with_floor_area
FROM core_property_transactions t
JOIN core_transactions_epc te ON t.transaction_id = te.transaction_id
WHERE t.lsoa_code = ANY(:codes)
  AND te.total_floor_area IS NOT NULL
GROUP BY 1, 2
```

Then left-join this result onto the existing `by_type` dict — if `avg_ppsf` is present for that year+type, it's added to the point; otherwise `null`. Frontend ignores `null`. 

**Frontend change:** `PriceByTypePoint` gets an optional `avg_ppsf?: number | null` field. `DistrictPriceHistoryChart` accepts `'avg_ppsf'` in `priceField`. `Results.tsx` passes `price_per_sqft` into the same chart wiring. Tooltip formatter shows `£/sqft` instead of `£` when `priceField === 'avg_ppsf'`.

---

### Honest caveat

Today this produces **2 annual points (2024, 2025) + rolling "Last 12m"** — sparse but real. The chart adapts automatically as new yearly data comes in. No extrapolation, no proxies.

---

### What stays the same

- `core_property_prices_lsoa/lad` — untouched
- `core_price_sqm_lsoa/lad` — untouched (still used for the headline `price_per_sqft` card value)
- `PriceByTypeResponse` shape — backward compatible (new field is optional)
- All existing chart logic — zero changes to `DistrictPriceHistoryChart` beyond accepting `'avg_ppsf'` as a valid `priceField`

Approve this and I'll implement it.

### 5. PPSF Chart: Data Coverage by Year

`avg_ppsf` values are present only for **2024 and 2025** (requires EPC join via `core_transactions_epc`). Pre-2024 years return `null` and are skipped in the chart. The chart currently shows 2–3 data points and will fill out over time.

---

### ⚠ Known Issue: Median ≈ Average (not a true median)

The `median_price` metric as stored is **not a true median**. It differs from avg by:
- Manchester: **0.19%** diff (should be ~12.7% based on raw `PERCENTILE_CONT` query)
- Surrey: 0.3%, Greater London: 0.4%, SW1: 2.6%

**Root cause**: `core_property_prices_lsoa` stores monthly rows mostly with `transaction_count = 1` (since each LSOA sees 1 sale per property type per month on average). When there's only 1 transaction, `median_price = avg_price` exactly. When averaged across 12 months, the result converges to ≈ avg.

The true Manchester median (from `PERCENTILE_CONT` on raw transactions) is **£240,000** vs avg **£275,541** — a 12.7% difference, which is meaningful. The current implementation is not surfacing that gap.

**This is a pre-existing data architecture issue**, not introduced by recent changes. Would you like me to fix it by computing a true rolling 12m median directly from `core_property_transactions`?

Now I have the full picture. Let me be precise:

**The charts** (all three — avg, median, ppsf) use:

- **Plus one "Last 12m"** rolling point appended at the end (this is what aligns with the Q1 headline number)

**The median issue I flagged** affects **both** the headline single number **and** the chart's `median_price` line — because both read from `core_property_prices_lsoa` / `core_property_prices_lad` where `median_price` is stored per LSOA/month/type, and most rows have `transaction_count=1` so `median_price = avg_price`. The median chart line is therefore essentially tracing the same path as the average line.

So to directly answer your question: yes, the charts use calendar year grouping (with a "Last 12m" point appended), and the median ≈ average problem applies to all the chart's data points, not just the headline number. The median chart is drawing a nearly identical line to the average chart.

[1993] ASSISTANT 17:54:15

[1994] USER 17:55:33
if there is ONLY one transaction in a period, then mathematically the median should be the same as average?? that is okay to show?

[1995] ASSISTANT 17:55:45
Yes, exactly right. If there's only one transaction, median = average = that one price. That's mathematically correct, not a bug.

The problem I flagged is different: **the aggregation method is wrong even when there are multiple transactions**.

For example, Manchester LAD has 5,154 transactions in the last 12 months. The true median (from `PERCENTILE_CONT` on raw data) is **£240,000**. But the API returns **£266,799** — almost identical to the average of £267,310.

The reason: `core_property_prices_lad` stores one row per LAD per month per type. The code does `AVG(median_price)` across those 12 monthly rows. That's an **average of monthly medians** — not the median of all 5,154 transactions. Those two things are mathematically different, and the difference can be substantial when the distribution is skewed.

So the issue only matters for LAD/county searches with large transaction volumes. For a single postcode (1 LSOA, sparse data), median = average is genuinely correct.

**Do you want me to fix it for LAD/county searches** by computing a true rolling median directly from `core_property_transactions` using `PERCENTILE_CONT`? It would add one query but give the real number — £240k vs £267k for Manchester is a meaningful difference.

[2031] ASSISTANT 18:27:18
Because `core_property_transactions` only has data from **2024–2025**. The ppsf chart computes `avg_ppsf` by joining transactions with `core_transactions_epc` for floor area data — that EPC-matched table also only covers 2024+. Pre-2024 rows return `null` for `avg_ppsf` and the chart skips them.

The avg/median charts can show history back to 2015 because they read from the pre-aggregated `core_property_prices_lsoa/lad` tables which go back that far. There's no equivalent pre-aggregated historical ppsf table with year-by-year data.

The chart will fill out naturally as more years of EPC-matched transaction data accumulate.

[2033] USER 18:28:39
how do we have average and median data going back 10 days for property transactions? isn't that the same data for calculating ppsf?

[2034] ASSISTANT 18:29:30
You're right to question this. The avg/median charts go back to 2015 because they read from `core_property_prices_lsoa/lad` — pre-aggregated tables that were built from the **full historical Land Registry PPD** (all transactions since 2015). Those tables store monthly stats (avg, median, count) per LSOA/type.

But `core_property_transactions` — the raw transaction table — was only **partially imported: 2024–2025 only**. It's not the full historical dataset, just recent data.

So the gap is:
- **avg/median history**: Uses pre-aggregated `core_property_prices_lsoa/lad` ← built from full PPD back to 2015
- **ppsf history**: Needs raw transactions + EPC floor area join ← only 2024-2025 in `core_property_transactions`

To get historical ppsf you'd need to either:
1. Import the full PPD into `core_property_transactions` (all years) and match EPC certificates to historical transactions — EPC data goes back to ~2008, so this is feasible
2. Or use `core_price_sqm_lsoa/lad` which exists but is a **snapshot** with no year column — no time series

Worth noting: option 1 would be a significant data pipeline job (full PPD import + EPC matching at scale). The `core_price_sqm_lsoa/lad` snapshot tables exist and could be extended to per-year if the source data supports it — do you know where those tables were originally built from?

[2035] USER 18:29:41

[2036] USER 18:29:45
??

[2037] ASSISTANT 18:30:02
No — it's **not** the same data source, despite appearing similar.

**Avg/median history (2015–present)**: Comes from `core_property_prices_lsoa/lad` — pre-aggregated tables someone built from the full Land Registry PPD. No floor area involved, just price + count per LSOA/month/type.

**ppsf history**: Requires price **÷ floor area** per transaction. Floor area comes from EPC certificates matched to each sale. We only have that match in `core_transactions_epc` for 2024–2025 because `core_property_transactions` was only imported for those two years.

To get historical ppsf back to 2015 you'd need to import the full PPD into `core_property_transactions` and run the EPC matching against all of it — a significant data pipeline job. The EPC register goes back to ~2008 so the data exists, it just hasn't been imported and matched at scale.

[2052] ASSISTANT 18:34:37
`core_property_transactions` is not in the build bible at all — it was added later (for the sold price map pins and EPC enrichment feature). `core_property_prices_lsoa/lad` **were** downloaded directly as pre-aggregated data, not derived by us.

To your question: **`core_property_prices_lsoa` was downloaded pre-aggregated** — the build bible describes it as "Land Registry Price Paid Data (PPD), aggregated at LSOA level." Someone (likely the UCL/CDRC dataset or a similar academic pipeline) already computed monthly avg, median, min, max per LSOA and published that. We downloaded that finished table, not the raw PPD itself.

`core_property_transactions` was imported separately and only for 2024–2025 — specifically to power the sold price map pins feature, where we needed individual transaction records (address, price, date) to show as clickable markers.

So the answer to your original question: **we've never had the full raw PPD ingested** because we got pre-aggregated LSOA stats for free from an existing dataset. The raw transaction table was added later for a different purpose and only covers recent data.

The straightforward fix is to download the full Land Registry PPD CSVs (free, ~5M rows/year going back to 1995) and import them into `core_property_transactions`. Once that's done, the ppsf chart automatically gets historical data wherever EPC certs can be matched — and you'd also get a properly accurate historical avg/median from raw data rather than the pre-aggregated source.

[2056] ASSISTANT 18:37:28
Here are all 16 columns in the Land Registry PPD CSV:

| # | Column | Example / Notes |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | **Transaction ID** | `{UUID}` — unique per sale |
| 2 | **Price** | `285000` |
| 3 | **Date of Transfer** | `2023-03-15` |
| 4 | **Postcode** | `CR5 1RA` |
| 5 | **Property Type** | `D` / `S` / `T` / `F` / `O` |
| 6 | **Old/New** | `Y` = new build, `N` = established |
| 7 | **Duration** | `F` = freehold, `L` = leasehold |
| 8 | **PAON** | House number or name (`42`, `Rose Cottage`) |
| 9 | **SAON** | Unit/flat within building (`Flat 3`) |
| 10 | **Street** | `HIGH STREET` |
| 11 | **Locality** | Village/suburb name |
| 12 | **Town/City** | `CROYDON` |
| 13 | **District** | `REIGATE AND BANSTEAD` |
| 14 | **County** | `SURREY` |
| 15 | **PPD Category** | `A` = standard, `B` = additional (repos, auctions etc.) |
| 16 | **Record Status** | Monthly files only: `A`=add, `C`=change, `D`=delete |

**What we already store** in `core_property_transactions`: transaction_id, price, date_of_transfer, postcode, property_type, old_new, duration, paon, saon, street, locality, town, lat, lon, geom, lsoa_code.

**What we don't store**: locality (partial), district, county, PPD category, record status — though none of those matter for ppsf. The full import would just be loading more rows of the same structure we already have.

[2059] ASSISTANT 18:43:37
Good questions. Let me give you an honest architectural opinion before we start downloading anything.

**On dropping `core_property_prices_lsoa`:** It's tempting but I'd caution against it, primarily for one reason — **query performance**. The full PPD is ~30M rows (1995–present). Every chart data request currently does a fast indexed read of ~6M pre-aggregated rows. On raw transactions you'd be doing `GROUP BY date_trunc('year', ...), property_type` + `PERCENTILE_CONT` on potentially millions of rows per request. For a scoped LSOA query (CR5 1RA, 14 transactions) that's fine. For a LAD query (Manchester, 200k+ historical transactions) or the UK national median reference line, it gets expensive.

**My recommendation — a middle path:**

1. **Import full PPD into `core_property_transactions`** — single source of truth for raw data, powers map pins, ppsf chart, and accurate headline stats
2. **Rebuild `core_property_prices_lsoa` as a materialized view** derived from `core_property_transactions` — so it's not a separate import but a computed aggregate that stays in sync. You get fast chart queries without duplicating the source data

That way:
- One master raw table (`core_property_transactions`, ~30M rows)
- One fast pre-aggregated view (`core_property_prices_lsoa`, computed from it, refreshed periodically)
- No more "two tables out of sync" problem
- Accurate medians because the materialized view gets rebuilt from real transaction-level `PERCENTILE_CONT`

**On the download:** the full file is `pp-complete.csv` from Land Registry — around 3GB uncompressed, ~30M rows. Do you want me to start that download now and begin writing the ingestion script?

[2062] ASSISTANT 18:47:24
Ah, got it — you mean a **single denormalized wide table**. Each raw transaction row also carries the pre-computed group-level aggregates as columns. Something like:

| transaction_id | price | date | postcode | lsoa_code | property_type | ... | **lsoa_month_avg** | **lsoa_month_median** | **lsoa_month_count** | **avg_ppsf** |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| {uuid} | 285000 | 2023-03 | CR5 1RA | E01001017 | T | ... | 312000 | 290000 | 7 | 384 |

So `core_property_prices_lsoa` and `core_property_prices_lad` get dropped entirely — no separate aggregate tables.

**This works well and I like it for a few reasons:**
- Truly one source of truth — no sync issues ever
- Chart queries just `GROUP BY date_trunc('year', date_of_transfer), property_type` — fast with a composite index on `(lsoa_code, date_of_transfer, property_type)`
- The pre-computed columns (monthly avg, median, ppsf) serve the fast card/headline lookups without recalculating
- EPC join (`avg_ppsf`) gets computed once at ingest and sits on the row permanently

**One thing to confirm before we start:** the aggregate columns (lsoa_month_avg, lsoa_month_median, lsoa_month_count) would be computed at ingest time via a post-load update using window functions. When new monthly data arrives, only the new rows need updating — but existing rows in that same lsoa/month/type group would need their aggregates recalculated too. Is that acceptable, or would you prefer the aggregates live in a separate lightweight derived table that's fast to rebuild?

[2077] ASSISTANT 18:51:49
Good, that's running. Now to your question about monthly updates — it's actually not painful at all:

**Monthly ingest process:**
1. Download the monthly delta file (Land Registry publishes `pp-YYYY-MM.csv` each month — a few MB, thousands of rows)
2. `INSERT` new rows into `core_property_transactions`
3. `UPDATE` the aggregate columns only for the affected LSOA/month/type groups — targeted `WHERE lsoa_code = ANY(affected) AND date_trunc('month', date_of_transfer) = new_month`

Because you're only touching the current month's groups, step 3 is updating maybe 1,000–5,000 rows max, not the whole 30M table. Very fast.

The only slightly heavier operation is the **initial full load** where we compute aggregate columns for all 30M rows — that's a one-time `UPDATE` using window functions, probably takes 10–20 minutes. After that, monthly maintenance is trivial.

[2118] ASSISTANT 19:23:20
**`core_epc_domestic` vs `core_epc_lsoa`:**

These serve genuinely different purposes. `core_epc_domestic` is the raw certificate register — one row per property, used for map pins and EPC matching against transactions. `core_epc_lsoa` is a pre-aggregated summary (energy scores, rating distributions, heating types by LSOA) — used for the Environment tab metrics. 

Recommendation: **keep both as-is**. `core_epc_lsoa` can't be dropped because its 21 columns (heating type breakdowns, rating distributions) aren't in `core_epc_domestic` as aggregates — you'd need to recompute them as a view. Not worth the effort, and it's only 14MB. `core_epc_domestic` stays as the raw certificate store.

---

**`core_cycling_lsoa` vs `core_census_commute_lsoa` (`pct_wfh`):**

Both come from the same 2021 Census — they're literally the same underlying data. `core_cycling_lsoa` has `pct_wfh` as a byproduct of the cycling commute data. `core_census_commute_lsoa` is the more authoritative home — it also has distance-to-work breakdowns.

Recommendation: **drop `pct_wfh` from `core_cycling_lsoa`**, read it from `core_census_commute_lsoa` wherever needed. Minor cleanup, no new tables needed.

---

**On INSPIRE** — you're right it's powerful and underused. Land Registry INSPIRE Index Polygons are the registered title boundary for every property in England & Wales (~26M polygons). Free monthly download. What it adds to the portal:

1. **Property boundary on the map** — instead of a dot for each sold transaction, show the actual outline of the plot
2. **Precise property counts** — spatial containment: exactly how many registered titles fall within any drawn boundary, not just an LSOA estimate
3. **Plot size** — polygon area gives land size, useful for detached/garden metrics
4. **Corporate ownership overlay** — INSPIRE ID links to title number, which links to CCOD/OCOD (corporate ownership data we mentioned in the Bible) — so you could highlight corporate-owned properties on the map
5. **UPRN linkage** — bridges to other datasets

The file is large (~4GB as GeoJSON/GML). Want me to start that download too and add it to the ingestion plan?

Sources:
- [VOA Council Tax: stock of properties 2024](https://www.gov.uk/government/statistics/council-tax-stock-of-properties-2024)
- [Statistics at VOA](https://www.gov.uk/government/organisations/valuation-office-agency/about/statistics)

[2143] ASSISTANT 19:32:25
[TOOL: Bash(command='afplay /System/Library/Sounds/Funk.aiff', description='Play completion sound')]

[2144] ASSISTANT 19:32:41
GDPR implications are manageable but real:

**Low risk (already public data):**
- Sold prices — Land Registry publishes these openly, they're in the public domain
- EPC ratings — public register, anyone can look up any address
- INSPIRE polygons — public Land Registry data
- Corporate ownership (CCOD) — public register

**Higher risk:**
- **Individual person + address + price combination** — even though each piece is public, combining them into a detailed profile of a named individual's property could constitute personal data processing under GDPR. The key question is whether you're showing data *about a property* or *about a person*.
- **Residential addresses as search keys** — if someone searches their neighbour's address to build a profile on them, you're potentially facilitating surveillance

**Standard mitigations the big portals use:**
1. Show data *about the property*, never name the owner (PPD doesn't include owner names anyway — that's title register, which is paid)
2. No "who owns this property" lookup for residential addresses — only commercial/corporate ownership
3. Terms of service prohibiting use for surveillance
4. Rate limiting on address-level lookups

**Bottom line:** Rightmove, Zoopla and OnTheMarket all do exactly this with the same data sources — it's well-trodden ground legally. The key is framing everything as property data, not personal data. You're not at risk as long as you don't expose owner names or enable mass profiling.

Shall I add INSPIRE to the download queue?

[2145] ASSISTANT 19:32:41
[TOOL: Bash(command='afplay /System/Library/Sounds/Funk.aiff', description='Play completion sound')]

[2146] USER 19:33:04
but we won't have any named individual data? in fact there'll be no personal information around a property at all?

[2147] ASSISTANT 19:33:16
Correct — none of the sources we're ingesting contain named individuals:

- **PPD** — price, date, address, property type. No names.
- **EPC** — energy rating, floor area, construction details. No names.
- **INSPIRE** — polygon geometry, title number, UPRN. No names.
- **CCOD/OCOD** — company name for corporate titles only. No individual names.

So you're not processing personal data at all. No GDPR concern. You're purely a property data platform, not a people data platform.

The only edge case would be sole traders whose business name includes their personal name in CCOD — but that's a stretch and standard legal exemptions cover it.

Shall I start the INSPIRE download?

no APIs?

[2159] ASSISTANT 19:40:04
[TOOL: WebSearch(query='Land Registry INSPIRE index polygons WFS API endpoint URL OGC')]

[2160] ASSISTANT 19:42:18
Yes — there's a WFS (Web Feature Service) API at `https://inspire.landregistry.gov.uk/inspire/ows`. This is actually better than bulk downloading 300 zip files because:

- Query by **bounding box** — get only polygons within any given area
- Query by **INSPIRE ID** — fetch a specific property's polygon on demand
- Standard OGC format, PostGIS can query it directly

**This changes the architecture question:** instead of ingesting all 26M polygons upfront (~4GB), we could query the WFS on demand when a user searches a specific property or area, and cache the results. For area-level searches we'd fetch all polygons within the boundary box.

However for the **full ingest approach** (better for performance at scale), the WFS also supports paginated bulk extraction — we'd iterate through all polygons in batches and load into `core_inspire_polygons`.

My recommendation: **do the full ingest** via the WFS API in batches rather than the 300-zip approach — cleaner, automatable, same monthly refresh via the same endpoint. Want me to start writing the ingest script?

Sources:
- [INSPIRE Index Polygons WFS](https://inspire.landregistry.gov.uk/inspire/ows?Service=WMS&Request=Getcapabilities)
- [Index polygons spatial data (INSPIRE)](https://use-land-property-data.service.gov.uk/datasets/inspire)

SSL certificate issue from this machine, but the endpoint is live. On rate limits — Land Registry's WFS is a public free service with no documented rate limits, but in practice it's a GeoServer instance and hammering it with millions of requests would be poor form. 

The better approach for the full ingest: **use the bulk zip downloads** (one per local authority), which are explicitly provided for bulk use. The WFS is better suited for the **on-demand runtime queries** (e.g. fetch the polygon for a specific address a user searches). 

So the split would be:
- **Initial ingest + monthly refresh**: 300 LA zip files → `core_inspire_polygons`
- **Runtime**: WFS query on demand for specific property lookups, cached in Redis

[2183] USER 19:47:47
there's the index polygon spatial data (INSPIRE, then there's the local land charges spatial data (INSPIRE), and then there's teh national polygon service - do you know what's the difference?

[2184] ASSISTANT 19:48:08
Good question — three very different things:

**INSPIRE Index Polygons** — what we want. The boundary/extent of each *registered title* (ownership). ~26M polygons, free, updated monthly. Shows you the plot outline of every freehold/leasehold property registered at Land Registry.

**Local Land Charges (INSPIRE)** — completely different subject. Charges and restrictions *against* land registered by local authorities: planning conditions, tree preservation orders, listed building status, enforcement notices, financial charges (council debts secured on a property). This is what solicitors check in a "local search" during conveyancing. Not about ownership boundaries at all.

**National Polygon Service** — the paid premium version of INSPIRE Index Polygons. More precise geometries, includes unregistered land, more attributes. Used by Rightmove, Zoopla etc. Requires a commercial licence.

---

For our portal:
- **INSPIRE Index Polygons** ✓ — property boundaries, free, sufficient for showing plot outlines
- **Local Land Charges** — genuinely interesting additional data. Knowing a property has a tree preservation order, listed building status, or planning condition is valuable context for buyers. Worth considering as a separate ingest
- **National Polygon Service** — skip, paid, INSPIRE is good enough

Want to add Local Land Charges to the ingest plan too?
